			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Sugar</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." /><img id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" class="fr-toggle-arrow" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;" alt="show/hide details" /></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" class="flaggedrevs_short_details" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Sugar">reviewed</a> on <i>14 March 2017</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the class of sweet-flavored substances used as food. For common table sugar, see <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">Sucrose</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Sugar_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Sugar (disambiguation)">Sugar (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg/330px-Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg" width="330" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg/495px-Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg/660px-Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1696" data-file-height="1392" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raw_sugar_closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Closeup of raw crystals of unrefined, unbleached sugar</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Sugar</b> is the generalized name for sweet, short-chain, soluble <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>, many of which are used in food, which are composed of carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen. There are various types of sugar derived from different sources. Simple sugars are called <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> and include <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (also known as dextrose), <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>. The "table sugar" or "granulated sugar" most customarily used as food is <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharide</a> of glucose and fructose. Sugar is used in prepared foods (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cookies" class="mw-redirect" title="Cookies">cookies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cake" title="Cake">cake</a>) and it is added to some foods and beverages (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Oatmeal" title="Oatmeal">oatmeal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>). In the body, sucrose is hydrolysed into fructose and glucose. Other disaccharides include <a href="/wiki/Maltose" title="Maltose">maltose</a> from <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malted</a> grain, and <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> from <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>. Longer chains of sugars are called <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Oligosaccharides">oligosaccharides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">polysaccharides</a>. Some other chemical substances, such as <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> may also have a sweet taste, but are not classified as sugars. <a href="/wiki/Low-calorie" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-calorie">Low-calorie</a> food substitutes for sugar, described as <a href="/wiki/Artificial_sweetener" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial sweetener">artificial sweeteners</a>, include <a href="/wiki/Aspartame" title="Aspartame">aspartame</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sucralose" title="Sucralose">sucralose</a>, a chlorinated derivative of sucrose.</p>
<p>Sugars are found in the tissues of most plants, but are present in sufficient concentrations for efficient extraction only in <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sugarcane refers to any of several species, or their hybrids, of giant grasses in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum" title="Saccharum">Saccharum</a></i> that have been cultivated in tropical climates in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> since ancient times. A great expansion in its production took place in the 18th century with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Slave_plantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave plantation">slave plantations</a> in the Americas. The use of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> meant that this was the first time that sugar became cheap enough for most people, who previously had to rely on honey to sweeten foods. Sugar beet, a <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivated variety</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Beta_vulgaris" title="Beta vulgaris">Beta vulgaris</a></i>, is grown as a root crop in cooler climates and became a major source of sugar in the 19th century when methods for extracting the sugar became available. Sugar production and trade have changed the course of human history in many ways, influencing the formation of colonies, the perpetuation of slavery, the transition to <a href="/wiki/Indentured_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured labour">indentured labour</a>, the migration of peoples, wars between sugar-trade–controlling states in the 19th century, and the ethnic composition and political structure of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>.</p>
<p>The world produced about 168 million tonnes of sugar in 2011. The average person consumes about 24 kilograms (53&#160;lb) of sugar each year (33.1&#160;kg in <a href="/wiki/Industrialised_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialised countries">industrialised countries</a>), equivalent to over 260 food calories per person, per day. Since the latter part of the twentieth century, it has been questioned whether a diet high in sugars, especially refined sugars, is good for human health. Sugar has been linked to obesity, and suspected of, or fully implicated as a cause in the occurrence of diabetes, cardiovascular disease, dementia, macular degeneration, and tooth decay. Numerous studies have been undertaken to try to clarify the position, but with varying results, mainly because of the difficulty of finding populations for use as controls that do not consume or are largely free of any sugar consumption.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg/220px-Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg/330px-Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg/440px-Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg 2x" data-file-width="775" data-file-height="394" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Myrmicaria_brunnea.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">Ant</a> feeding on sugar crystals.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> reflects the spread of the commodity. The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> word "sugar" ultimately originates from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">शर्करा</span> (<i>śarkarā</i>), via <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">سكر</span> (<i>sukar</i>) as granular or candied sugar, which is cognate with the Greek word, <i>kroke</i>, or "pebble".<sup id="cite_ref-oed_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-1">[1]</a></sup> (the <i>g</i> is unexplained, possibly a <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">Venetian</a> dialect). The contemporary Italian word is <i>zucchero</i>, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> words, <i>azúcar</i> and <i>açúcar</i>, respectively, have kept a trace of the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_definite_article" title="Arabic definite article">Arabic definite article</a>. The Old French word is <i>zuchre</i> and the contemporary French, <i>sucre</i>. The earliest <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word attested is <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">σάκχαρις</span> (<i>sákkʰaris</i>).</p>
<p>The English word <i><a href="/wiki/Jaggery" title="Jaggery">jaggery</a></i>, a coarse brown sugar made from <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> sap or <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> juice, has a similar etymological origin – Portuguese <i>jagara</i> from the Sanskrit <span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">शर्करा</span> (<i>śarkarā</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_sugar" title="History of sugar">History of sugar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_times_and_Middle_Ages">Ancient times and Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient times and Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/220px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/330px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/440px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1880" data-file-height="1253" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">Sugar cane</a> plantation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sugar has been produced in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> since ancient times. It was not plentiful or cheap in early times and <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> was more often used for sweetening in most parts of the world. Originally, people chewed raw sugarcane to extract its sweetness. Sugarcane was a native of tropical <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kiple_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiple-4">[4]</a></sup> Different species seem to have originated from different locations with <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_barberi" title="Saccharum barberi">Saccharum barberi</a></i> originating in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_edule" title="Saccharum edule">S. edule</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_officinarum" title="Saccharum officinarum">S. officinarum</a></i> coming from <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kiple_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiple-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharpe_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharpe-5">[5]</a></sup> One of the earliest historical references to sugarcane is in Chinese manuscripts dating back to 8th century BC that state that the use of sugarcane originated in India.<sup id="cite_ref-gr1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gr1-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sugar was found in Europe by the 1st century AD, but only as an imported medicine, and not as a food.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-faas_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faas-8">[8]</a></sup> The Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Dioscorides" class="mw-redirect" title="Dioscorides">Dioscorides</a> in the 1st century (AD) described sugar in his medical treatise <a href="/wiki/De_Materia_Medica" title="De Materia Medica">De Materia Medica</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, a 1st-century (AD) Roman, described sugar in his <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a>: "Sugar is made in Arabia as well, but Indian sugar is better. It is a kind of honey found in cane, white as gum, and it crunches between the teeth. It comes in lumps the size of a hazelnut. Sugar is used only for medical purposes."<sup id="cite_ref-faas_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faas-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sugar remained relatively unimportant until the Indians discovered methods of turning <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane_juice" title="Sugarcane juice">sugarcane juice</a> into granulated crystals that were easier to store and to transport.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas-10">[10]</a></sup> Crystallized sugar was discovered by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Gupta dynasty">Imperial Guptas</a>, around the 5th century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas-10">[10]</a></sup> In the local Indian language, these crystals were called <i>khanda</i> (<a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a>:खण्ड,<span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Khaṇḍa</span>), which is the source of the word <i>candy</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Indian sailors, who carried <a href="/wiki/Clarified_butter" title="Clarified butter">clarified butter</a> and sugar as supplies, introduced knowledge of sugar on the various <a href="/wiki/Trade_routes" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade routes">trade routes</a> they travelled.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas-10">[10]</a></sup> Buddhist monks, as they travelled around, brought sugar crystallization methods to China.<sup id="cite_ref-Kieschnick1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kieschnick1-12">[12]</a></sup> During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Harsha" title="Harsha">Harsha</a> (r. 606–647) in <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>, Indian envoys in <a href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang China</a> taught methods of cultivating sugarcane after <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> (r. 626–649) made known his interest in sugar. China then established its first sugarcane plantations in the seventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-sen_38_40_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sen_38_40-13">[13]</a></sup> Chinese documents confirm at least two missions to India, initiated in 647 AD, to obtain technology for sugar refining.<sup id="cite_ref-Kieschnick11_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kieschnick11-14">[14]</a></sup> In South Asia, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, sugar became a staple of cooking and <a href="/wiki/Dessert" title="Dessert">desserts</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusaders</a> brought sugar home with them to Europe after their campaigns in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>, where they encountered caravans carrying "sweet salt". Early in the 12th century, Venice acquired some villages near <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a> and set up estates to produce sugar for export to Europe, where it supplemented honey, which had previously been the only available sweetener.<sup id="cite_ref-Ponting_2000_481_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ponting_2000_481-15">[15]</a></sup> Crusade chronicler <a href="/wiki/William_of_Tyre" title="William of Tyre">William of Tyre</a>, writing in the late 12th century, described sugar as "very necessary for the use and health of mankind".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> was the chief sugar refining and distribution centre in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-gr1_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gr1-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg/220px-George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg/330px-George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg/440px-George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="824" data-file-height="1050" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Still-Life" class="mw-redirect" title="Still-Life">Still-Life</a> with Bread and Confectionery</i>, by <a href="/wiki/George_Flegel" class="mw-redirect" title="George Flegel">George Flegel</a>, first half of 17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">Triangular trade</a></div>
<p>In August 1492, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> stopped at <a href="/wiki/La_Gomera" title="La Gomera">La Gomera</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, for wine and water, intending to stay only four days. He became romantically involved with the governor of the island, <a href="/wiki/Beatriz_de_Bobadilla_y_Ossorio" title="Beatriz de Bobadilla y Ossorio">Beatriz de Bobadilla y Ossorio</a>, and stayed a month. When he finally sailed, she gave him cuttings of sugarcane, which became the first to reach the New World.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The first sugar cane harvest was conducted in Hispaniola in 1501, and many sugar mills had been constructed in Cuba and Jamaica by the 1520s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenitez-Rojo199693_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenitez-Rojo199693-18">[18]</a></sup> The Portuguese took sugar cane to Brazil. By 1540, there were 800 cane sugar mills in Santa Catarina Island and another 2,000 on the north coast of Brazil, Demarara, and Surinam.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/220px-Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/330px-Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/440px-Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="918" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Hacienda La Fortuna.</i> A sugar mill complex in Puerto Rico, painted by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Oller" title="Francisco Oller">Francisco Oller</a> in 1885. <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sugar was a luxury in Europe until the 18th century, when it became more widely available. It then became popular and by the 19th century, sugar came to be considered a necessity. This evolution of taste and demand for sugar as an essential food ingredient unleashed major economic and social changes.<sup id="cite_ref-mintz_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mintz-19">[19]</a></sup> It drove, in part, colonization of tropical islands and nations where labor-intensive sugarcane plantations and sugar manufacturing could thrive. The demand for cheap labor to perform the hard work involved in its cultivation and processing increased the demand for the slave trade from Africa (in particular West Africa). After slavery was abolished, there was high demand for <a href="/wiki/Indentured_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured labor">indentured laborers</a> from South Asia (in particular India).<sup id="cite_ref-britain1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britain1-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Millions of slave and indentured laborers were brought into the Caribbean and the Americas, Indian Ocean colonies, southeast Asia, Pacific Islands, and East Africa and Natal. The modern ethnic mix of many nations that have been settled in the last two centuries has been influenced by the demand for sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sugar also led to some <a href="/wiki/Industrialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a> of areas where sugar cane was grown. For example, Lieutenant J. Paterson, of the Bengal establishment, persuaded the British Government that sugar cane could be cultivated in British India with many advantages and at less expense than in the West Indies; as a result, sugar factories were established in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a> in eastern India.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, sugar beet production increased in continental Europe because of the difficulty of importing sugar when shipping was subject to <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockade</a>. By 1880, the sugar beet was the main source of sugar in Europe. It was cultivated in Lincolnshire and other parts of England, although the United Kingdom continued to import the main part of its sugar from its colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until the late nineteenth century, sugar was purchased in <a href="/wiki/Sugarloaf" title="Sugarloaf">loaves</a>, which had to be cut using implements called <a href="/wiki/Sugar_nips" title="Sugar nips">sugar nips</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In later years, granulated sugar was more usually sold in bags. Sugar cubes were produced in the nineteenth century. The first inventor of a process to make sugar in cube form was the <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravian</a> <a href="/wiki/Jakub_Kry%C5%A1tof_Rad" title="Jakub Kryštof Rad">Jakub Kryštof Rad</a>, director of a sugar company in <a href="/wiki/Da%C4%8Dice" title="Dačice">Dačice</a>. He began sugar cube production after being granted a five-year patent for the process on January 23, 1843. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Tate" title="Henry Tate">Henry Tate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tate_%26_Lyle" title="Tate &amp; Lyle">Tate &amp; Lyle</a> was another early manufacturer of sugar cubes at his refineries in Liverpool and London. Tate purchased a patent for sugar cube manufacture from German <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Langen" title="Eugen Langen">Eugen Langen</a>, who in 1872 had invented a different method of processing of sugar cubes.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saccharose.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Saccharose.svg/320px-Saccharose.svg.png" width="320" height="160" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="160" /></a>

<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">Carbohydrate</a></div>
<p>Scientifically, <i>sugar</i> loosely refers to a number of carbohydrates, such as monosaccharides, disaccharides, or <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharides</a>. Monosaccharides are also called "simple sugars," the most important being glucose. Almost all sugars have the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
n</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2n</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
n</span></span> (n is between 3 and 7). <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a> has the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_formula" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular formula">molecular formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
12</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span></span>. The names of typical sugars end with -<i>ose</i>, as in "<a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>". Sometimes such words may also refer to any types of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> soluble in <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Open-chain_compound" title="Open-chain compound">acyclic</a> mono- and disaccharides contain either <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehyde</a> groups or <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketone</a> groups. These <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbonyl">carbon-oxygen</a> double bonds (C=O) are the reactive centers. All saccharides with more than one ring in their structure result from two or more monosaccharides joined by <a href="/wiki/Glycosidic_bond" title="Glycosidic bond">glycosidic bonds</a> with the resultant loss of a molecule of water (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span>) per bond.<sup id="cite_ref-Pigman_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigman-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monosaccharides in a closed-chain form can form glycosidic bonds with other monosaccharides, creating disaccharides (such as sucrose) and polysaccharides (such as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>). <a href="/wiki/Enzymes" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzymes">Enzymes</a> must hydrolyze or otherwise break these glycosidic bonds before such compounds become <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolized</a>. After digestion and absorption the principal monosaccharides present in the blood and internal tissues include glucose, fructose, and galactose. Many pentoses and hexoses can form <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_compound" title="Heterocyclic compound">ring structures</a>. In these closed-chain forms, the aldehyde or ketone group remains non-free, so many of the reactions typical of these groups cannot occur. Glucose in solution exists mostly in the ring form at <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">equilibrium</a>, with less than 0.1% of the molecules in the open-chain form.<sup id="cite_ref-Pigman_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigman-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_polymers">Natural polymers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Natural polymers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">Biopolymers</a> of sugars are common in nature. Through photosynthesis, plants produce <a href="/wiki/Glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate">glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate</a> (G3P), a phosphated 3-carbon sugar that is used by the cell to make monosaccharides such as glucose (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
12</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span></span>) or (as in cane and beet) sucrose (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
12</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
22</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
11</span></span>). Monosaccharides may be further converted into <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides#structural_polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">structural polysaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a> construction or into energy reserves in the form of <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides#storage_polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">storage polysaccharides</a> such as starch or <a href="/wiki/Inulin" title="Inulin">inulin</a>. Starch, consisting of two different polymers of glucose, is a readily degradable form of chemical <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">energy</a> stored by <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, and can be converted to other types of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Pigman_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigman-30">[30]</a></sup> Another polymer of glucose is cellulose, which is a linear chain composed of several hundred or thousand glucose units. It is used by plants as a structural component in their cell walls. Humans can digest cellulose only to a very limited extent, though <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a> can do so with the help of <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> bacteria in their gut.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> are built up of the monosaccharides <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a>, respectively. Deoxyribose has the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
5</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
10</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> and ribose the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
5</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
10</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
5</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability">Flammability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Flammability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because sugars burn easily when exposed to flame, the handling of sugars risks <a href="/wiki/Dust_explosion" title="Dust explosion">dust explosion</a>. The <a href="/wiki/2008_Georgia_sugar_refinery_explosion" title="2008 Georgia sugar refinery explosion">2008 Georgia sugar refinery explosion</a>, which killed 14 persons and injured 40, and destroyed most of the refinery, was caused by the ignition of sugar dust.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugar_2xmacro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Sugar_2xmacro.jpg/310px-Sugar_2xmacro.jpg" width="310" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Sugar_2xmacro.jpg/465px-Sugar_2xmacro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Sugar_2xmacro.jpg/620px-Sugar_2xmacro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugar_2xmacro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Magnification of grains of refined <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, the most common free sugar.</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monosaccharides">Monosaccharides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Monosaccharides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fructose, galactose, and glucose are all simple sugars, <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a>, with the general formula C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>. They have five hydroxyl groups (−OH) and a carbonyl group (C=O) and are cyclic when dissolved in water. They each exist as several <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomers</a> with dextro- and laevo-rotatory forms that cause polarized light to diverge to the right or the left.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">Fructose</a></b>, or fruit sugar, occurs naturally in fruits, some root vegetables, cane sugar and honey and is the sweetest of the sugars. It is one of the components of sucrose or table sugar. It is used as a <a href="/wiki/High-fructose_corn_syrup" title="High-fructose corn syrup">high-fructose syrup</a>, which is manufactured from hydrolyzed corn starch that has been processed to yield <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a>, with enzymes then added to convert part of the glucose into fructose.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>In general, <b><a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a></b> does not occur in the free state but is a constituent with glucose of the disaccharide <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> or milk sugar. It is less sweet than glucose. It is a component of the antigens found on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a> that determine <a href="/wiki/ABO_blood_group_system" title="ABO blood group system">blood groups</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Raven_and_Johnson_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raven_and_Johnson-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a></b>, dextrose or grape sugar, occurs naturally in fruits and plant juices and is the primary product of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Most ingested carbohydrates are converted into glucose during digestion and it is the form of sugar that is transported around the bodies of animals in the bloodstream. It can be manufactured from starch by the addition of enzymes or in the presence of acids. Glucose syrup is a liquid form of glucose that is widely used in the manufacture of foodstuffs. It can be manufactured from starch by <a href="/wiki/Enzymatic_hydrolysis" title="Enzymatic hydrolysis">enzymatic hydrolysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disaccharides">Disaccharides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Disaccharides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lactose, maltose, and sucrose are all compound sugars, <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharides</a>, with the general formula C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>22</sub>O<sub>11</sub>. They are formed by the combination of two monosaccharide molecules with the exclusion of a molecule of water.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">Lactose</a></b> is the naturally occurring sugar found in milk. A molecule of lactose is formed by the combination of a molecule of galactose with a molecule of glucose. It is broken down when consumed into its constituent parts by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Lactase" title="Lactase">lactase</a> during digestion. Children have this enzyme but some adults no longer form it and they are unable to digest lactose.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Maltose" title="Maltose">Maltose</a></b> is formed during the germination of certain grains, the most notable being <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, which is converted into <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a>, the source of the sugar's name. A molecule of maltose is formed by the combination of two molecules of glucose. It is less sweet than glucose, fructose or sucrose.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-33">[33]</a></sup> It is formed in the body during the digestion of starch by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a> and is itself broken down during digestion by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Maltase" title="Maltase">maltase</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">Sucrose</a></b> is found in the stems of sugarcane and roots of sugar beet. It also occurs naturally alongside fructose and glucose in other plants, in particular fruits and some roots such as carrots. The different proportions of sugars found in these foods determines the range of sweetness experienced when eating them.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-33">[33]</a></sup> A molecule of sucrose is formed by the combination of a molecule of glucose with a molecule of fructose. After being eaten, sucrose is split into its constituent parts during digestion by a number of enzymes known as <a href="/wiki/Sucrase" title="Sucrase">sucrases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sugars" title="List of sugars">List of sugars</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sugar_beet">Sugar beet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sugar beet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg/170px-Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg/255px-Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg/340px-Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2244" data-file-height="2820" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Packed_sugar_Slutsk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A pack of sugar made of sugar beet.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">Sugar beet</a> (<i>Beta vulgaris</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Biennial_plant" title="Biennial plant">biennial plant</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Amaranthaceae" title="Amaranthaceae">Amaranthaceae</a>, the tuberous root of which contains a high proportion of sucrose. It is cultivated in temperate regions with adequate rainfall and requires a fertile soil. The crop is harvested mechanically in the autumn and the crown of leaves and excess soil removed. The roots do not deteriorate rapidly and may be left in a clamp in the field for some weeks before being transported to the processing plant. Here the crop is washed and sliced and the sugar extracted by diffusion. <a href="/wiki/Milk_of_lime" class="mw-redirect" title="Milk of lime">Milk of lime</a> is added to the raw juice and <a href="/wiki/Carbonatation" title="Carbonatation">carbonatated</a> in a number of stages in order to purify it. Water is evaporated by boiling the syrup under a vacuum. The syrup is then cooled and seeded with sugar crystals. The white sugar that crystallizes out can be separated in a centrifuge and dried. It requires no further refining.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sugarcane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</a> (<i>Saccharum spp.</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> grass in the family <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Poaceae</a>. It is cultivated in tropical and sub-tropical regions for the sucrose that is found in its stems. It requires a frost-free climate with sufficient rainfall during the growing season to make full use of the plant's great growth potential. The crop is harvested mechanically or by hand, chopped into lengths and conveyed rapidly to the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar mill">processing plant</a> (commonly known as a <a href="/wiki/Sugar_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar mill">sugar mill</a>). Here, it is either milled and the juice extracted with water or extracted by diffusion. The juice is then clarified with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">lime</a> and heated to destroy <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. The resulting thin syrup is concentrated in a series of evaporators, after which further water is removed by evaporation in vacuum containers. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">supersaturated</a> solution is seeded with sugar crystals and the sugar crystallizes out and is separated from the fluid and dried. <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">Molasses</a> is a by-product of the process and the fiber from the stems, known as <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>, is burned to provide energy for the sugar extraction process. The crystals of raw sugar have a sticky brown coating and either can be used as they are or can be bleached by <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> or can be treated in a <a href="/wiki/Carbonatation" title="Carbonatation">carbonatation</a> process to produce a whiter product.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> About 2,500 litres (660&#160;US&#160;gal) of irrigation water is needed for every one kilogram of sugar produced.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refining">Refining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Refining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sugar_refinery" title="Sugar refinery">Sugar refinery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg/220px-Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg/330px-Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg/440px-Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1149" data-file-height="1068" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sugars; clockwise from top-left:
<center>White refined, unrefined,<br />
brown, unprocessed cane</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Refined sugar is made from raw sugar that has undergone a <a href="/wiki/Refining" title="Refining">refining</a> process to remove the <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-agrifinasi_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agrifinasi-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kompas_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kompas-46">[46]</a></sup> Raw sugar is <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> which is extracted from sugarcane or <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>. While raw sugar can be consumed, the refining process removes unwanted tastes and results in refined sugar or white sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-western_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-western-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tebu_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tebu-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The sugar may be transported in bulk to the country where it will be used and the refining process often takes place there. The first stage is known as affination and involves immersing the sugar crystals in a concentrated syrup that softens and removes the sticky brown coating without dissolving them. The crystals are then separated from the liquor and dissolved in water. The resulting syrup is treated either by a <a href="/wiki/Carbonatation" title="Carbonatation">carbonatation</a> or by a phosphatation process. Both involve the precipitation of a fine solid in the syrup and when this is filtered out, many of the impurities are removed at the same time. Removal of colour is achieved by using either a granular <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Ion-exchange_resin" title="Ion-exchange resin">ion-exchange resin</a>. The sugar syrup is concentrated by boiling and then cooled and seeded with sugar crystals, causing the sugar to crystallize out. The liquor is spun off in a centrifuge and the white crystals are dried in hot air and ready to be packaged or used. The surplus liquor is made into refiners' molasses.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_for_Uniform_Methods_of_Sugar_Analysis" title="International Commission for Uniform Methods of Sugar Analysis">International Commission for Uniform Methods of Sugar Analysis</a> sets standards for the measurement of the purity of refined sugar, known as ICUMSA numbers; lower numbers indicate a higher level of purity in the refined sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG/180px-Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG" width="180" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG/270px-Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG/360px-Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raw_cane_sugar_light.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unrefined sugar</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Refined sugar is widely used for industrial needs for higher quality. Refined sugar is purer (ICUMSA below 300) than raw sugar (ICUMSA over 1,500).<sup id="cite_ref-tebu_2_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tebu_2-51">[51]</a></sup> The level of purity associated with the colors of sugar, expressed by standard number <a href="/wiki/ICUMSA" class="mw-redirect" title="ICUMSA">ICUMSA</a> (International Commission for Uniform Methods of sugar Analysis), the smaller ICUMSA numbers indicate that higher purity of sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-tebu_2_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tebu_2-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_countries">Producing countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Producing countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The five largest producers of sugar in 2011 were <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, India, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, China and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. In the same year, the largest exporter of sugar was Brazil, distantly followed by Thailand, Australia and India. The largest importers were the European Union, United States and Indonesia. At present, Brazil has the highest <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> consumption of sugar, followed by Australia, Thailand, and the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-Sugar:_World_Markets_and_Trade_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sugar:_World_Markets_and_Trade-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-consumption_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-consumption-53">[53]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_and_uses">Forms and uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Forms and uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg/170px-Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg" width="170" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg/255px-Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg/340px-Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1790" data-file-height="2760" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rock_candy" title="Rock candy">Rock candy</a> crystallized out of a <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">supersaturated sugar solution</a> that contains green dye.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">Brown sugars</a> are granulated sugars, either containing residual molasses, or with the grains deliberately coated with molasses to produce a light- or dark-colored sugar. They are used in baked goods, confectionery, and <a href="/wiki/Toffee" title="Toffee">toffees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Granulated sugars are used at the table, to sprinkle on foods and to sweeten hot drinks (coffee and tea), and in home baking to add sweetness and texture to baked products (cookies and cakes) and desserts (pudding and ice cream). They are also used as a preservative to prevent micro-organisms from growing and perishable food from spoiling, as in candied fruits, jams, and <a href="/wiki/Marmalade" title="Marmalade">marmalades</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inverted_sugar_syrup" title="Inverted sugar syrup">Invert sugars</a> and syrups are blended to manufacturers specifications and are used in breads, cakes, and beverages for adjusting sweetness, aiding moisture retention and avoiding crystallization of sugars.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Liquid sugars are strong syrups consisting of 67% granulated sugar dissolved in water. They are used in the food processing of a wide range of products including beverages, <a href="/wiki/Hard_candy" title="Hard candy">hard candy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">jams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Low-calorie sugars and artificial sweeteners are often made of <a href="/wiki/Maltodextrin" title="Maltodextrin">maltodextrin</a> with added sweeteners. Maltodextrin is an easily digestible synthetic <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a> consisting of short chains of glucose molecules and is made by the partial <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> of starch. The added sweeteners are often <a href="/wiki/Aspartame" title="Aspartame">aspartame</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saccharin" title="Saccharin">saccharin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stevia" title="Stevia">stevia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sucralose" title="Sucralose">sucralose</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>Milled sugars (known as confectioner's sugar and powdered sugar) are ground to a fine powder. They are used as <a href="/wiki/Powdered_sugar" title="Powdered sugar">powdered sugar</a> (also known as icing sugar or confectionary sugar), for dusting foods and in baking and <a href="/wiki/Confectionery" title="Confectionery">confectionery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Molasses is commonly used to make <a href="/wiki/Rum" title="Rum">rum</a>, and sugar byproducts are used to make <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> for fuel.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Polyol" title="Polyol">Polyols</a> are <a href="/wiki/Sugar_alcohol" title="Sugar alcohol">sugar alcohols</a> and are used in chewing gums where a sweet flavor is required that lasts for a prolonged time in the mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-Union1990_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Union1990-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>Screened sugars are crystalline products separated according to the size of the grains. They are used for decorative table sugars, for blending in dry mixes and in baking and confectionery.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sugar cubes (sometimes called sugar lumps) are white or brown granulated sugars lightly steamed and pressed together in block shape. They are used to sweeten drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sugarloaf" title="Sugarloaf">Sugarloaf</a> was the usual <a href="/wiki/Cone" title="Cone">cone</a>-form in which refined sugar was produced and sold until the late 19th century. This shape is still in use in Germany (for preparation of <i><a href="/wiki/Feuerzangenbowle" title="Feuerzangenbowle">Feuerzangenbowle</a></i>), in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syrup" title="Syrup">Syrups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treacle" title="Treacle">treacles</a> are dissolved invert sugars heated to develop the characteristic flavors. (Treacles have added molasses.) They are used in a range of baked goods and confectionery including toffees and <a href="/wiki/Licorice" class="mw-redirect" title="Licorice">licorice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_54-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugars_in_wine" title="Sugars in wine">fruit sugars</a> are converted into <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermentation</a> process. If the <a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a> formed by pressing the fruit has a low sugar content, additional sugar may be added to raise the alcohol content of the wine in a process called <a href="/wiki/Chaptalization" title="Chaptalization">chaptalization</a>. In the production of sweet wines, fermentation may be halted before it has run its full course, leaving behind some <a href="/wiki/Residual_sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Residual sugar">residual sugar</a> that gives the wine its sweet taste.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_pg_665-666_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_pg_665-666-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In most parts of the world, sugar is an important part of the human diet, making food more palatable and providing <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>. After cereals and vegetable oils, sugar derived from sugarcane and beet provided more kilocalories per capita per day on average than other food groups.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/FAO" class="mw-redirect" title="FAO">FAO</a>, an average of 24 kilograms (53&#160;lb) of sugar, equivalent to over 260 food calories per day, was consumed annually per person of all ages in the world in 1999. Even with rising human populations, sugar consumption is expected to increase to 25.1 kilograms (55&#160;lb) per person per year by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-fao2002p_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao2002p-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Data collected in multiple U.S. surveys between 1999 and 2008 show that the intake of <a href="/wiki/Added_sugars" class="mw-redirect" title="Added sugars">added sugars</a> has declined by 24 percent with declines occurring in all age, ethnic and income groups.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
