<p>The per capita consumption of refined sugar in the United States has varied between 27 and 46 kilograms (60 and 101&#160;lb) in the last 40 years. In 2008, American per capita total consumption of sugar and sweeteners, exclusive of artificial sweeteners, equalled 61.9&#160;kg (136&#160;lb) per year. This consisted of 29.65&#160;kg (65.4&#160;lb) pounds of refined sugar and 31&#160;kg (68.3&#160;lb) pounds of corn-derived sweeteners per person.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Health effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Studies examining the health impact of sugars are inconclusive. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Meta-analyses" class="mw-redirect" title="Meta-analyses">meta-analyses</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> studies showed contrasting impacts of sugar in refined and unrefined forms.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003-65">[65]</a></sup> Other studies indicated variable results between health effects, particularly on <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, and whether the research was funded by the sugar industry or those by independent sponsors.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT01_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT01-66">[66]</a></sup> The 'empty calories' argument is that a diet high in added sugar will reduce consumption of foods that contain essential nutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Addiction">Addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sugar addiction is the term for the relationship between sugar and the various aspects of <a href="/wiki/Food_addiction" title="Food addiction">food addiction</a> including "<a href="/wiki/Binge_eating" title="Binge eating">bingeing</a>, withdrawal, craving and cross-sensitization". Some scientists assert that consumption of <a href="/wiki/Sweets" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweets">sweets</a> or sugar could have a <a href="/wiki/Opioid_dependence" class="mw-redirect" title="Opioid dependence">heroin addiction</a>-like effect,<sup id="cite_ref-Avena-Rada-Hoebel_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Avena-Rada-Hoebel-68">[68]</a></sup> but recent reviews suggest that sugar addiction does not occur in humans.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alzheimer.27s_disease">Alzheimer's disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Alzheimer's disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Claims have been made of a sugar–<a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> connection, but debate continues over whether cognitive decline is attributable to dietary fructose or to overall energy intake.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_glucose_levels">Blood glucose levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Blood glucose levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbohydrates are classified according to their <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_index" title="Glycemic index">glycemic index</a>, a system for measuring how quickly a food that is eaten raises blood sugar levels, and <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_load" title="Glycemic load">glycemic load</a>, which takes into account both the glycemic index and the amount of carbohydrate in the food.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This has led to carbohydrate counting, a method used by diabetics for planning their meals.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiovascular_disease">Cardiovascular disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cardiovascular disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Studies in animals have suggested that chronic consumption of refined sugars can contribute to metabolic and <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular dysfunction</a>. Some experts have suggested that refined fructose is more damaging than refined glucose in terms of cardiovascular risk.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Cardiac performance has been shown to be impaired by switching from a carbohydrate diet including fiber to a high-carbohydrate diet.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Switching from saturated <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> to carbohydrates with high <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_index" title="Glycemic index">glycemic index</a> values shows a statistically-significant increase in the risk of <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Other studies have shown that the risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">coronary heart disease</a> is decreased by adopting a diet high in polyunsaturated fatty acids but low in sugar, whereas a low-fat, high-carbohydrate diet brings no reduction. This suggests that consuming a diet with a high <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_load" title="Glycemic load">glycemic load</a> typical of the "<a href="/wiki/Junk_food" title="Junk food">junk food</a>" diet is strongly associated with an increased risk of developing coronary heart disease.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The consumption of added sugars has been positively associated with multiple measures known to increase cardiovascular disease risk amongst adolescents as well as adults.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Studies are suggesting that the impact of refined carbohydrates or high glycemic load carbohydrates are more significant than the impact of saturated fatty acids on cardiovascular disease.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> A high dietary intake of sugar (in this case, sucrose or disaccharide) can substantially increase the risk of heart and vascular diseases. According to a Swedish study of <span class="nowrap">4301 people</span> undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Lund_University" title="Lund University">Lund University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malm%C3%B6_University_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Malmö University College">Malmö University College</a>, sugar was associated with higher levels of bad <a href="/wiki/Blood_lipids" title="Blood lipids">blood lipids</a>, causing a high level of small and medium <a href="/wiki/Low-density_lipoprotein" title="Low-density lipoprotein">low-density lipoprotein</a> (LDL) and reduced <a href="/wiki/High-density_lipoprotein" title="High-density lipoprotein">high-density lipoprotein</a> (HDL). In contrast, the amount of fat eaten did not affect the level of blood fats. Incidentally quantities of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> were linked to an increase in the good HDL blood fat.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hyperactivity">Hyperactivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Hyperactivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is a common notion that sugar leads to hyperactivity, in particular in children, but studies and meta-studies question or address this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Consumers_Union_of_U.S_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consumers_Union_of_U.S-83">[83]</a></sup> Some articles and studies do refer to the increasing evidence supporting the links between refined sugar and hyperactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The WHO FAO meta-study suggests that such inconclusive results are to be expected when some studies do not effectively segregate or control for free sugars as opposed to sugars still in their natural form (entirely unrefined) while others do.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003-65">[65]</a></sup> One study followed thirty-five 5-to-7-year-old boys who were reported by their mothers to be behaviorally "sugar-sensitive." They were randomly assigned to <a href="/wiki/Treatment_and_control_groups" title="Treatment and control groups">experimental and control groups</a>. In the experimental group, mothers were told that their children were fed sugar, and, in the control group, mothers were told that their children received a <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>. In fact, all children received the placebo, but mothers in the sugar expectancy condition rated their children as significantly more hyperactive.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This result suggests that the real effect of sugar is that it increases worrying among parents with preconceived notions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obesity_and_diabetes">Obesity and diabetes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Obesity and diabetes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Controlled trials have now shown unequivocally that consumption of sugar-sweetened beverages increases body weight and body fat, and that replacement of sugar by artificial sweeteners reduces weight.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Studies on the link between sugars and diabetes are inconclusive, with some suggesting that eating excessive amounts of sugar does not increase the risk of <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a>, although the extra calories from consuming large amounts of sugar can lead to <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, which may itself increase the risk of developing this <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolic disease">metabolic disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dietetic_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dietetic-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-diabetes_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diabetes-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Other studies show correlation between refined sugar (also called "free sugar") consumption and the onset of diabetes, and negative correlation with the consumption of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">fiber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> These included a 2010 meta-analysis of eleven studies involving 310,819 participants and 15,043 cases of <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">type&#160;2 diabetes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>This found that "SSBs (<a href="/wiki/Sweetened_beverage" title="Sweetened beverage">sugar-sweetened beverages</a>) may increase the risk of <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a> and type&#160;2 diabetes not only through obesity but also by increasing dietary glycemic load, leading to <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">β-cell</a> dysfunction, and inflammation". As an overview to consumption related to chronic disease and obesity, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>'s independent meta-studies specifically distinguish free sugars ("all monosaccharides and disaccharides added to foods by the manufacturer, cook or consumer, plus sugars naturally present in honey, syrups and fruit juices") from sugars occurring naturally in food. The reports prior to 2000 set the limits for free sugars at a maximum of 10% of carbohydrate intake, measured by energy, rather than mass, and since 2002 have aimed for a level across the entire population of less than 10%.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003-65">[65]</a></sup> The consultation committee recognized that this goal is "controversial. However, the Consultation considered that the studies showing no effect of free sugars on excess weight have limitations".<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_Expert_Consultation_2003-65">[65]</a></sup> A 2015 <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> report noted that "a review of beverage studies, published in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/PLOS_Medicine" title="PLOS Medicine">PLOS Medicine</a></i>, found that those funded by <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a>, <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Beverage_Association" title="American Beverage Association">American Beverage Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Association" title="Sugar Association">sugar industry</a> were five times more likely to find no link between sugary drinks and weight gain than studies whose authors reported no financial conflicts."<sup id="cite_ref-NYT01_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT01-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tooth_decay">Tooth decay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tooth decay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In regard to contributions to <a href="/wiki/Dental_caries" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental caries">tooth decay</a>, the role of free sugars is also recommended to be below an absolute maximum of 10% of energy intake, with a minimum of zero. There is "convincing evidence from human intervention studies, epidemiological studies, animal studies and experimental studies, for an association between the amount and frequency of free sugars intake and dental caries" while other sugars (complex carbohydrate) consumption is normally associated with a lower rate of dental caries.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-102">[102]</a></sup> Lower rates of tooth decay have been seen in individuals with <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_fructose_intolerance" title="Hereditary fructose intolerance">hereditary fructose intolerance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19723928_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19723928-103">[103]</a></sup> Also, studies have shown that the consumption of sugar and starch have different impacts on oral health with the ingestion of starchy foods and fresh fruit being associated with low levels of dental caries.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recommended_dietary_intake">Recommended dietary intake</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Recommended dietary intake">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization_(WHO)" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organization (WHO)">World Health Organization (WHO)</a> recommends<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_2015_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_2015-104">[104]</a></sup> that both adults and children reduce the intake of free sugars to less than 10% of total energy intake. A reduction to below 5% of total energy intake brings additional health benefits, especially in what regards dental <a href="/wiki/Caries" class="mw-redirect" title="Caries">caries</a> (cavities in the teeth). These recommendations were based on the totality of available evidence reviewed regarding the relationship between free sugars intake and body weight and dental caries. Free sugars include monosaccharides and disaccharides added to foods and beverages by the manufacturer, cook or consumer, and sugars naturally present in honey, syrups, fruit juices and fruit juice concentrates.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_2015_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_2015-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 20, 2016 the U.S. Food and Drug Administration announced changes to the Nutrition Facts panel displayed on all foods, to be effective by July 2018. New to the panel is a requirement to list "Added sugars" by weight and as a percent of Daily Value (DV). For vitamins and minerals the intent of DVs is to indicate how much should be consumed. For added sugars, the guidance is that 100% DV should not be exceeded. 100% DV is defined as 50 grams. For a person consuming 2000 calories a day, 50 grams, the amount to not exceed, is the same as 200 calories, and thus 10% of total calories - same guidance as the World Health Organization.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> To put this into context, most 12 ounce (335 mL) cans of soda contain 39 grams of sugar. In the United States, a recently published government survey on food consumption reported that for men and women ages 20 and older the average total sugar intakes - naturally occurring in foods and added - were, respectively, 125 and 99 g/day. <sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurements">Measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Various culinary sugars have different densities due to differences in particle size and inclusion of moisture.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Domino_Sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Domino Sugar">Domino Sugar</a> gives the following weight to volume conversions (in <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">United States customary units</a>):<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Firmly packed brown sugar 1&#160;lb = 2.5 cups (or 1.3 L per kg, 0.77&#160;kg/L)</li>
<li>Granulated sugar 1&#160;lb = 2.25 cups (or 1.17 <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">L</a> per <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a>, 0.85&#160;kg/L)</li>
<li>Unsifted confectioner's sugar 1&#160;lb = 3.75 cups (or 2.0 L per kg, 0.5&#160;kg/L)</li>
</ul>
<p>The "Engineering Resources – Bulk Density Chart" published in <i>Powder and Bulk</i> gives different values for the bulk densities:<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Beet sugar 0.80 g/mL</li>
<li>Dextrose sugar 0.62 g/mL ( = 620&#160;kg/m^3)</li>
<li>Granulated sugar 0.70 g/mL</li>
<li>Powdered sugar 0.56 g/mL</li>
</ul>
<p><span id="History"></span></p>
